    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3739362381869975099"><i>I Wish Someone Were Waiting For Me Somewhere</i> explores how a life can be changed irrevocably in just one fateful moment. A pregnant mother's plans for the future unravel at the hospital; a travelling salesman learns the consequences of an almost-missed exit on the motorway in the newspaper the next morning; while a perfect date is spoilt by a single act of thoughtlessness. </span>
  <span id="freeText3739362381869975099" style="display:none"><i>I Wish Someone Were Waiting For Me Somewhere</i> explores how a life can be changed irrevocably in just one fateful moment. A pregnant mother's plans for the future unravel at the hospital; a travelling salesman learns the consequences of an almost-missed exit on the motorway in the newspaper the next morning; while a perfect date is spoilt by a single act of thoughtlessness. In those crucial moments Gavalda demonstrates her almost magical skill in conveying love, lust, longing, and loneliness. <br /><br /><i>Someone I Loved</i> is a hauntingly intimate look at the intolerably painful, yet sometimes valuable consequences that adultery can have on a marriage and the individuals involved. A simple tale, yet long in substance, <i>Someone I Loved</i> ends like most great love affairs, forever leaving you wanting just one more moment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3739362381869975099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4172990307071031397"><b>Je Voudrais Que Quelqu'un M'attende Quelque Part = I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere, Anna Gavalda</b> <br/><br/>I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere was first published in 1999 under the title <b>Je Voudrais Que Quelqu'un M'attende Quelque Part</b> that met with both critical acclaim and commercial success, selling more than three-quarters of a million copies in her native France and winning the 2000 Grand Prix RTL-Lire.<br/><br/>عنوانها: «دوست داشتم کسی جایی منتظرم باشد»؛ «کاش کسی جایی منتظرم باشد»؛ نویسن</span>
  <span id="freeText4172990307071031397" style="display:none"><b>Je Voudrais Que Quelqu'un M'attende Quelque Part = I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere, Anna Gavalda</b> <br /><br />I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere was first published in 1999 under the title <b>Je Voudrais Que Quelqu'un M'attende Quelque Part</b> that met with both critical acclaim and commercial success, selling more than three-quarters of a million copies in her native France and winning the 2000 Grand Prix RTL-Lire.<br /><br />عنوانها: «دوست داشتم کسی جایی منتظرم باشد»؛ «کاش کسی جایی منتظرم باشد»؛ نویسنده: آنا گاوالدا؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بستم ماه ژانویه سال 2007میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دوست داشتم کسی جایی منتظرم باشد؛ نویسنده: آنا گاوالدا؛ مترجم: الهام دارچینیان؛ تهران، قطره، 1386؛ در 198ص؛ شابک 9643416119؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان فرانسوی - <div id="review_345005163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345005163" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369500256p2/3695758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345005163?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3695758-suzanne?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345005163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10993952956151939396">This is what it means to write with economy. All of these short stories – and most of them are very short— are told in the first person. No flowery metaphors here. The voices do the heavy lifting, creating these little gems that are revealing and poignant. What Gavalda does with so few words is amazing. While many are emotionally affecting and thought-provoking, there is humor too. The end of “Junior” is the funniest thing I’ve read in a while. <br/><br/>There is much said on Goodreads about the unreliabi</span>
  <span id="freeText10993952956151939396" style="display:none">This is what it means to write with economy. All of these short stories – and most of them are very short— are told in the first person. No flowery metaphors here. The voices do the heavy lifting, creating these little gems that are revealing and poignant. What Gavalda does with so few words is amazing. While many are emotionally affecting and thought-provoking, there is humor too. The end of “Junior” is the funniest thing I’ve read in a while. <br /><br />There is much said on Goodreads about the unreliability of first person narrators, as though it were a formulaic rule that a first-person narrative is <u>always</u> unreliable. I don’t think that’s necessarily true, at least no more so than it is for people telling stories in real life, who are sometimes less than forthcoming for various good or bad reasons, or self-deluded, or simply don’t have all the information. I also think it’s not as important as whether or not a narrator is self-aware. The very fact of the narrator’s self-awareness is what makes several of this stories work so well. They know what their problem is. Not that it changes anything. But they know. Other times their character traits, and not necessarily undesirable ones, lead them in directions that will wreck their lives, but circumstances have tripped them up. But character is fate. Deal with it. <br /><br />My favorites among the dozen stories in this volume include “Courting Rituals of the Saint-Germain-des- Pres,” “Amber,” “Leave,” “Lead Story,” “Catgut,” and “Junior.” <br /><br />The simplicity and the economy with which Gavalda tells a complete, plot-driven, arc-fully constructed story in very few pages, and the vivid voices used to do this, completely blew me away. I will be reading more of her. A shout-out, too, to translator Karen L. Marker.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10993952956151939396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41859866" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41859866" />
    <a title="Pris robichaud" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1860127-pris-robichaud"><img alt="Pris robichaud" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231079869p2/1860127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41859866?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pris robichaud" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pris robichaud" href="/user/show/1860127-pris-robichaud">Pris robichaud</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41859866" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10187544547575668553"><br/><br/> Is That So Much To Ask?, 5 Mar 2006 <br/> <br/><br/>"I shifted somewhat abruptly. She stood back up behind me and put both hands flat on my shoulders. She said, "I'm going to go. I want you not to move and not to turn around. Please I am begging of you. <br/>I didn't move I didn't want to anyway, because I didn't want her to see me with my eyes swollen and my face all contorted.<br/>I waited a while, and then headed to my car."<br/>Anna Gavalda has seen a lot in her life. This novel with twelve short stories of people tryin</span>
  <span id="freeText10187544547575668553" style="display:none"><br /><br /> Is That So Much To Ask?, 5 Mar 2006 <br /> <br /><br />"I shifted somewhat abruptly. She stood back up behind me and put both hands flat on my shoulders. She said, "I'm going to go. I want you not to move and not to turn around. Please I am begging of you. <br />I didn't move I didn't want to anyway, because I didn't want her to see me with my eyes swollen and my face all contorted.<br />I waited a while, and then headed to my car."<br />Anna Gavalda has seen a lot in her life. This novel with twelve short stories of people trying to connect shows her capturing their inner dialogues with perfect pitch. Reaching out, knowing they are missing something and moving to the center to find exactly what it is. These stories, all small snippets of life. Coming-apart love, met-again love, aborted love, unknown love and of love lost all woven from separate stories into a theme. <br /><br />The saddest is the "Pregnancy". The story of a new love and the nine months it takes to grow this new love, only to have an unknown enemy defeat it.<br /><br />In "For Years' a man searches for his lost love. Not reality searching but in his mind. He knows where she lives but not why she left. Both of them have moved on but he knows not why. And, then one day twelve years later he hears from her.<br /><br />In "Courting Rituals of the Saint-Germaine-des-Pres", an attractive woman smiles at a passing man, and he pursues her. He asks her out to dinner. They meet hours later, both with a purpose of their own. They are attracted to each other and she is thinking of what might happen after dinner, when all of a sudden his cell phone goes off. At that moment he shuts it off, but she sees him scrumptiously look a number of the caller. She knows that the evening is over, forever.<br /><br />each of these satires is better then the other, each one tells a separate but seemingly similar tale. These are the stories of a friend who is telling you bits and pieces of life. These are lively stories told with flourish and style. There is variety that surprises you, Parisian chic or hilarious, sophisticated farce. Anna Gavalda sees through ordinary appearances to our hidden longings. Highly Recommended. prisrob 3-4-06 <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10187544547575668553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText15103687057717412893" style="display:none">I was first attracted to the book by its title that I really liked and still do, I just loved the simplicity of it that means so much. But then I was really disappointed when I started reading it, it was BORING. <div id="review_4335851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4335851" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4335851?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/166376-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=read-in-2008">read-in-2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4335851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12679147772615707665">This collection of short stories by Anna Gavalda, a former high-school teacher and mother of two, won the Academie Francaise Short Story Award in 2000. The citation mentions the 'dry wit and almost involuntary elegance' apparent in the twelve stories. The translation (by Karen Marker, published in 2003) manages to preserve both the wit and elegance - these stories were fun to read. <br/><br/>Courting rituals of the Saint-Germain-de-Pres, a country vet's terrible revenge, or an indelible encounter between </span>
  <span id="freeText12679147772615707665" style="display:none">This collection of short stories by Anna Gavalda, a former high-school teacher and mother of two, won the Academie Francaise Short Story Award in 2000. The citation mentions the 'dry wit and almost involuntary elegance' apparent in the twelve stories. The translation (by Karen Marker, published in 2003) manages to preserve both the wit and elegance - these stories were fun to read. <br /><br />Courting rituals of the Saint-Germain-de-Pres, a country vet's terrible revenge, or an indelible encounter between a wild boar and a Jaguar which cannot end well; there are stories about longing and loss, a few almost unbearably poignant; others hilariously funny. Remarkably, in many cases, it's only in the last few lines that Gavalda reveals her hand.<br /><br />Many of the stories reach the level of Dorothy Parker at her best, which is to say they are very good indeed. Gavalda shifts easily between male and female perspectives; in almost every case the result is moving, without being sentimental, and is told with a dry, often devastating, wit. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12679147772615707665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_642310295" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/642310295" />
    <a title="Tiffany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16897950-tiffany"><img alt="Tiffany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359554258p2/16897950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/642310295?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tiffany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tiffany" href="/user/show/16897950-tiffany">Tiffany</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 3260050643016" href="/book/show/15705658-je-voudrais-que-quelqu-un-m-attende-quelque-part">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16897950-tiffany?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer642310295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3651440174272623490">I've always been fascinated by the French because they have this sneering way of communicating they are completely above mediocrity. It might be apocryphal, but I've bought into it. Remember that fabulous film "Diva"? The bald guy who dedicated his life to assembling a 10,000 piece puzzle? In <i>white</i>? He was so <i>cooooooooooooool</i> ... Loads of internal coolness. That white canvas was just a token of his Zen-like internal refrigeration. <br/><br/>It's possible I've been living under a misapprehension for most o</span>
  <span id="freeText3651440174272623490" style="display:none">I've always been fascinated by the French because they have this sneering way of communicating they are completely above mediocrity. It might be apocryphal, but I've bought into it. Remember that fabulous film "Diva"? The bald guy who dedicated his life to assembling a 10,000 piece puzzle? In <i>white</i>? He was so <i>cooooooooooooool</i> ... Loads of internal coolness. That white canvas was just a token of his Zen-like internal refrigeration. <br /><br />It's possible I've been living under a misapprehension for most of my life that the French are better than the rest of us because of their <i>exquisite</i> sensibilities. Did it take Nicholas Sarkozy to show us that underneath it all they're really just as <i>arriviste</i> as the rest of us? Certainly you'd think so reading Ms. Gavalda and her foray into the class-based social shake-out of France of the 90's and the aughts. This book is peopled with awful human beings who are completely caught up in their mundane and banal existences. The first of these stories--"I.I.G."--is about a woman newly incubating life. Her interior landscape is lunar at best. Her thoughts go like this: <blockquote>Bébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébébé...."</blockquote> ad nauseum. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When her baby dies and she is forced to give birth to a lifeless fetus, I was almost relieved that the train of her tedious, blank, baby-making blandness had been derailed. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Perhaps I owe something to Ms. Gavalda for finally stripping the scales from my eyes. The more I read the more I realize that this is precisely what she has set out to do: reveal the difference between what people playing their public roles think they are doing and how their actions really affect people around them. Most of these people are not striving for excellence, although they frequently dwell on their innate superiority. In "Permission" a young man is on leave from his military service. If ever there was a being living in a deluded world of judgment and otiosity this is he. He describes the presence of others around him as definitive proof there is no God. Could it be possible others are saying the same thing about him?<br /><br />"Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part" is uttered by the afore mentioned youthful blot. He's a sort of non-entity who doesn't fit in with his peers, his family, his society. While he's wrestling with his contempt for others he's also longing for recognition, belonging, community. Except it's just not cool to admit these things. It's much cooler to be an expressionless star-fucker-slash-photographer who is sleeping with such a messed up musician that the only thing on him she can find in working order is his hands. Yeah, not cool to want things. It's not cool to admit to longing for community--and yet, isn't it universal? What is Ms. Gavalda showing us? Is there evolution of understanding going on? Or has there always been this décalage between desire to be thought worthwhile and desire for being accepted warts and all? Has no popular novelist ever bothered to write about it? If this is the case then Ms. Gavalda is doing a much better job of unwinding my expectations because I started off scoffing at the lack of ornament in her writing.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part</i>
</b><br /><br />This title leans heavily on the subjunctive. The subjunctive tense is one of the best things about the French language (of which there are many; imagine having a special word for contradicting a negative question: "si!" It is just so clarifying and smart. Why can't we have one in English?!?!) The subjunctive is a verb tense that is largely obsolete in English although remnants of it still exist; when it is used it is invariably used incorrectly. I misuse it myself.<br /><br />The gift of the subjunctive is to hint (subtly) at truthfulness. This is not to say the French are the ultimately truthful race; they do, however, unarguably understand precision and nuance, which is why they are such good theoretical physicists and philosophers. Because the French grok that an idea, thought, belief, conviction, etc. is formless it can not be accepted as a fact. Thus one uses the subjunctive when something is not yet out of the realm of possibility. One also uses the subjunctive for prognostications, forecasts, and doubts. In English we indicate the precarious nature of these types of statements by using <i>aspects</i>, which is just the teeniest, tiniest bit less elegant than using a verb tense. An especially velvety use of the subjunctive is for necessity. <i>Il faut que</i> + subjunctive means "it's essential that ..." or "one must ..." Ha-ha-ha, those time traveling whip wielding language engineers of yore knew that just because <i>one must</i> doesn't mean <i>one will</i>. <br /><br /><br />Because Ms. Gavalda has decided to title her book with the inconclusive subjunctive, we must take everything we encounter there as tinged with possibility rather than probability. The young kid who distains others as much as he distains himself--does he really long to belong?; the heroin-shooting rapidly-aging musician--does he love the girl? or is it role-playing?; the god-awful B.C.B.G's driving their 60,000€ car with the wonky wiper--are they really so willing to swap having a real life for a nice weekend house with all the trimmings? ... I believe we are meant to wonder if they are all as rotten and blank and repulsive as they appear. <br /><br />---<br /><br />Ms. Gavalda has a certain amused contempt for people. She holds special distaste for her characters with class insecurity. I love how often her characters make sure you know they are not <i>ploucs</i>. ("Plouc" is onomatopoetic word for low life; only the French could give an actual sound to snobbery.) The story "Le Fait du Jour" was a soft-ball I saw coming--pleasurable nonetheless--and its companion "Pendant des Années" both play on middle-aged male solipsism. (Or is that a tautology?) "Cat-Gut" set me back on my feet. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Every woman in the world has at least once in her life plotted evil ways to get back at her aggressors--here for once we have a woman who has the means, motive and opportunity to do so and she does it creepily and well. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Her characters are always unlikeable but her stories have infinite pay-off. A special mention of"Junior", which, wow, I'm still chuckling over. Do read it then call up your aging parents and read it to them. <br /><br />From the way her books are splashed all over airports, I dove into this expecting that Ms. Gavalda was another Sophie Kinsella (note the use of subjunctive!) But no, she may actually be closer to Guy de Maupassant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3651440174272623490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_358931739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/358931739" />
    <a title="Her Royal Orangeness" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4708779-her-royal-orangeness"><img alt="Her Royal Orangeness" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392858770p2/4708779.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/358931739?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Her Royal Orangeness" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Her Royal Orangeness" href="/user/show/4708779-her-royal-orangeness">Her Royal Orangeness</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099506010" href="/book/show/1884291.I_Wish_Someone_Were_Waiting_for_Me_Somewhere">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4708779-her-royal-orangeness?shelf=author-gavalda">author-gavalda</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4708779-her-royal-orangeness?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4708779-her-royal-orangeness?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4708779-her-royal-orangeness?shelf=authors-i-heart">authors-i-heart</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358931739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17345346109906099195">“Life can be changed in just one fateful moment.” These are the words printed on the cover of “I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere” and they perfectly encapsulate the overarching theme that connects these stories.<br/><br/>The stories in this collection are, for the most part, startling in a very understated way. For example, “Lead Story,” my favorite in this collection, is about a man who makes a decision to do something that doesn’t seem particularly out-of-the-ordinary, but leads to a devastat</span>
  <span id="freeText17345346109906099195" style="display:none">“Life can be changed in just one fateful moment.” These are the words printed on the cover of “I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere” and they perfectly encapsulate the overarching theme that connects these stories.<br /><br />The stories in this collection are, for the most part, startling in a very understated way. For example, “Lead Story,” my favorite in this collection, is about a man who makes a decision to do something that doesn’t seem particularly out-of-the-ordinary, but leads to a devastating consequence. <br /><br />Anna Gavalda is gifted at creating characters. In just a few pages, she presented characters so well developed and complex that they seemed like real people. But what I admired most about the stories is how they lingered in my brain. The imagery was fresh in my mind for days after reading them, and I continued to puzzle over the subtle life questions that Gavalda poised.<br /><br />There were a few stories that I didn’t like much at all, but I think that was more a matter of a culture barrier than the quality of the writing. (Gavalda is a French author.) Overall, “I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere” is quite good and I enjoyed it. (3.5 Stars) </span>
  <a data-text-id="17345346109906099195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_171072886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171072886" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/285565-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315260462p2/285565.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171072886?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/285565-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171072886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18254159770108495461">This collection of short stories offers a good, satisfying mix of different voices, tones, and emotions. One of the things Anna Gavalda excels at is accurately reproducing the look and feel of our inner monologues to ourselves -- the self-doubt, guilt, awkwardness, fear, and muddle, but also the hopes, crushes, day dreams, inside jokes, and imagined futures that percolate through our brains on any given day. These stories also go by in a breeze that makes her writing seem effortless, but the the</span>
  <span id="freeText18254159770108495461" style="display:none">This collection of short stories offers a good, satisfying mix of different voices, tones, and emotions. One of the things Anna Gavalda excels at is accurately reproducing the look and feel of our inner monologues to ourselves -- the self-doubt, guilt, awkwardness, fear, and muddle, but also the hopes, crushes, day dreams, inside jokes, and imagined futures that percolate through our brains on any given day. These stories also go by in a breeze that makes her writing seem effortless, but the themes are heavy enough that she can never be accused of vapidness or silliness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18254159770108495461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_25967966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25967966" />

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25967966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7914337241729400669">A strange collection of short stories in that they're not quite quotable, but often incredibly lovely and poignant. Some of the stories fall a little flat but the ones that work work so well that it makes up for it ("Courting Rituals of the Saint-Germain-des-Pres," "Leave," "For Years," and "Clic-Clac").</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_163717494" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163717494" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5217151-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163717494?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/5217151-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5217151-lisa?shelf=francais">francais</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer163717494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5123237652800725249">The last book we read in French class as an example of contemporary, modern and popular literature. It is a collection of short stories, where the common themes usually are such as love and every day situations. Sometimes humour, sometimes tragedy. The titles is chosen, I believe, because "waiting" really is what the lives of all these people is marked by. Waiting for a lover, for a child to be born, to find a sense of belonging in life, to a letter from an editor to arrive... <br/><br/>I prefer the story</span>
  <span id="freeText5123237652800725249" style="display:none">The last book we read in French class as an example of contemporary, modern and popular literature. It is a collection of short stories, where the common themes usually are such as love and every day situations. Sometimes humour, sometimes tragedy. The titles is chosen, I believe, because "waiting" really is what the lives of all these people is marked by. Waiting for a lover, for a child to be born, to find a sense of belonging in life, to a letter from an editor to arrive... <br /><br />I prefer the story the title is taken from ("Permission") because it's kind of sweet and I can sort of relate to the main character in a way, even if it's really not that surprising. And I like the one called "Ambre", because it has a hopeful tone to it, despite the fact that the main character is a famous pop star who has lost big parts of his live to drugs. A bit chliché as it may be, I kind of like the idea of redemption through love and a second chance. <br /><br />The problem I see with it is that it's a bit banal. The writer wants to amuse and surprise but there are no real surprises and it's like everything is "ready-made", already served for the reader, too transparent. And most of the stories just don't interests me. The way the first one starts kind of proves my point, in a way:<br /><br /><blockquote>Saint-Germain-des-Prés!?... Je sais ce que vous allez me dire: "Mon Dieu, mais c'est d'un commun, ma chérie, Sagan l'a fait bien avant toi et tellllement mieux!" Je sais. </blockquote><br /><br />Here, Gavalda tries to disarm the reader with what can look like self-irony... I doesn't really work for me. I really think that there are more interesting stories to be found out there. <br /><br />The language was a bit hard for me to follow at times because it's very modern, colloquial even, but I think that's one reason why Gavalda is popular. Many people probably find it refreshing. Myself, I don't really like the style and the tone of voice of the writer... (I can say this because I read the book translated to Swedish a couple of years ago!) The reason I liked <i>Ensemble, c'est tout</i> a lot was probably mostly because I found the plot and the characters much more interesting.<br /><br />Still, I gave the book two stars because it wasn't crap... it's nice enough for a moment's diversion for example on an airplane, which was where I first read it. It's hard, if not impossible, to tell which of the authors of today that will be in tomorrow's books of the history of literature... Still, I think there must be more interesting collections of short stories to use as example of something "contemporary" in a French literature class.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5123237652800725249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_330007725" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/330007725" />
    <a title="Abeer Hoque" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82829-abeer-hoque"><img alt="Abeer Hoque" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442602533p2/82829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/330007725?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abeer Hoque" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abeer Hoque" href="/user/show/82829-abeer-hoque">Abeer Hoque</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82829-abeer-hoque?shelf=i-recommend">i-recommend</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer330007725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5855701813201050153">I wish the oceans didn't separate the lit worlds so. British books sometimes make it over this way, and sometimes translations of foreign language books, but not enough. So it took a hearty recommendation from a friend who lives in Paris for me to pick up this fabulous French book by Anna Gavalda: I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere, which took the French world by storm, and then went international. I myself devoured it in two days. <br/><br/>IWSWWfMS is a collection of stories, some so short as </span>
  <span id="freeText5855701813201050153" style="display:none">I wish the oceans didn't separate the lit worlds so. British books sometimes make it over this way, and sometimes translations of foreign language books, but not enough. So it took a hearty recommendation from a friend who lives in Paris for me to pick up this fabulous French book by Anna Gavalda: I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere, which took the French world by storm, and then went international. I myself devoured it in two days. <br /><br />IWSWWfMS is a collection of stories, some so short as to be vignettes, but don't imagine that narrative or character are sacrificed, not even for a split second. The personalities leap off the page, and the stories just get better and better (and they start off great). <br /><br />The range of characters is awesome, and their stories precisely and deeply described. The language is fresh and modern (perhaps a little too much so, but that's maybe my only complaint - it comes off almost slangy and careless sometimes - but maybe this is the translation). <br /><br />I can remember almost every one of the stories now, but the ones that jumped out at me included the one about the returning military soldier who falls for his brother's girlfriend, the one about a pregnancy, the one about a chance encounter between two would be lovers, the one about the two rich boys who take daddy's car out to a party, the one about a female vet working in a rural village, and the one about the sales rep whose mindless error changes his life. Oh I remember another complaint - unimaginative titles, so I won't bother looking them up for this review. But don't worry, you don't need them. You can read IWSWWfMS in one delicious shot, and you won't regret it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5855701813201050153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_56271091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56271091" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31082-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224474467p2/31082.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56271091?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy" href="/user/show/31082-andy">Andy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56271091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer890628786473052258">I liked this a lot. It seems like recently I've been reading collections of short stories just to shit on them, but I felt like these stories really justified the format. They had a zing or an oomph to them that I enjoyed very much.<br/><br/>I think that, in some ways, these stories appealed to the part of me that makes me tear up during romantic comedies (which is something I promised I wasn't going to volunteer anymore; oops). That's not to imply that I think they are just junk food or anything like tha</span>
  <span id="freeText890628786473052258" style="display:none">I liked this a lot. It seems like recently I've been reading collections of short stories just to shit on them, but I felt like these stories really justified the format. They had a zing or an oomph to them that I enjoyed very much.<br /><br />I think that, in some ways, these stories appealed to the part of me that makes me tear up during romantic comedies (which is something I promised I wasn't going to volunteer anymore; oops). That's not to imply that I think they are just junk food or anything like that. On the contrary, they were full of intelligence and wit and all sorts of disturbing things. Just that I think "'What are they about, your stories?' 'Everything.' 'Oh.' '...' '...' 'But mostly about love.' 'Oh?'" was a nice bit of metadescription.</span>
  <a data-text-id="890628786473052258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_8068530" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/8068530" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49416-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175707232p2/49416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8068530?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/49416-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8068530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16097152511673063102">This collection of short stories is as easy to read as pure fluff, but has substance and passion behind it. There are twelve stories in this book, all about ten pages long, which I gleefully devoured in an afteroon. The first-person narrators speak directly to their reader in conversational tones.<br/><br/>One of the things I love best about Gavalda's style is that she's sparse. Her pen-strokes are never wasted; no line of melodramatic adjectives strung on to fifty-cent words. She is at once precise and v</span>
  <span id="freeText16097152511673063102" style="display:none">This collection of short stories is as easy to read as pure fluff, but has substance and passion behind it. There are twelve stories in this book, all about ten pages long, which I gleefully devoured in an afteroon. The first-person narrators speak directly to their reader in conversational tones.<br /><br />One of the things I love best about Gavalda's style is that she's sparse. Her pen-strokes are never wasted; no line of melodramatic adjectives strung on to fifty-cent words. She is at once precise and vivid. <br /><br />I still favor her novel "Hunting and Gathering" (have yet to read "Someone I Loved") but am VERY satisfied by my most recent does of Gavalda.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16097152511673063102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

<span id="freeTextContainer15192236658183848425"> A collection of short stories set in Paris. <br/>We are allowed a glimpse into the -very- mundane life of the characters. <div id="review_374795494" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/374795494" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110724-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492186169p2/110724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/374795494?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/110724-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782290311783" href="/book/show/47775.Je_voudrais_que_quelqu_un_m_attende_quelque_part">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374795494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3613361171158137521">I raise my hand unceasingly in French class to ask exactly about words, but Gavalda is as good as any dictionary: <br/><br/>"A un moment, elle m'a demandé la difference entre désarroi (aimlessness) et désoeuvrement (confusion)."<br/><br/>And the moments of kindred spirit make sense:<br/><br/>"When New York Review of Books Classics republished Stoner, it was reviewed quite well, but sold modestly at first — until it caught the attention of Anna Gavalda, one of France's best-selling novelists. She had to read Stoner in English</span>
  <span id="freeText3613361171158137521" style="display:none">I raise my hand unceasingly in French class to ask exactly about words, but Gavalda is as good as any dictionary: <br /><br />"A un moment, elle m'a demandé la difference entre désarroi (aimlessness) et désoeuvrement (confusion)."<br /><br />And the moments of kindred spirit make sense:<br /><br />"When New York Review of Books Classics republished Stoner, it was reviewed quite well, but sold modestly at first — until it caught the attention of Anna Gavalda, one of France's best-selling novelists. She had to read Stoner in English — there wasn't a French translation — but she says she still felt a deep connection with the book.<br /><br />"I think it's a book I could have written myself because I feel really close to the author and the narrator, who, in my opinion are probably a bit of the same person," she said.<br /><br />Gavalda liked it so much that she asked her editor to buy the rights, so she could translate it herself. And the book took off.<br /><br />"My books sell really well in France," she explained, "so when all the other European editors saw that it was me who translated this book, they were all curious about why Anna Gavalda translated it, and so they all bought the rights."<br /><br />Back in New York, Frank can only speculate as to why Stoner has so moved European readers like Gavalda.<br /><br />"[Stoner] resonates I think, partly, because of the art with which the story has been told," he said. "So even as he sets the scene in Columbia, Missouri, at the same time, it could be anywhere."<br /><br />(from NPR: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.npr.org/2013/05/19/184770657/decade-later-and-across-an-ocean-a-novel-gets-its-due">http://www.npr.org/2013/05/19/1847706...</a>)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3613361171158137521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8596774132757777912" style="display:none">I never read short stories. It’s not that I don’t like them, I just appreciate novels more. I had to read a collection of short stories because of school. I liked the title and the cover was pretty, so I thought, why not this one? It’s short. Yeah, I hated this book. <br /><br />Gavalda’s stories are all almost pretty much the same. The characters have relationship issues, they all smoke, yearn for something that they don’t have. This book is SO french!! As a person who a) isn’t french b) has never been to france c) isn’t familiar with french culture, I didn’t get a lot of the stuff. <br /><br />All the characters were bland and forgettable. Nothing really happened in the stories. <br /><br />Also some things sounded a bit racist to me, like ’i hate african art, it all looks the same’. And describing pretty french girls as having milk white skin, like it’s their best quality and describing somebody as being ’pretty breed’. <br /><br />The stories were unnecessarily dirty, literally every chance the writer got she put a mention of body parts and sexual things. <br /><br />In conclusion : this book is so bad. Maybe I’m not french enough or miserable enough to understand marriage problems or vain enough to relate to the struggle of drinking not-fancy-enough wine. Or maybe I just prefer actually good stories?</span>

  <span id="freeText7327921567180113544" style="display:none">I don't remember the first short stories book I've ever read, so assumingly this is the first one. My first thought was, "This is strange." I guess I'm finally too used to fat books, which was why I felt funny when the first short story abruptly stopped. I told myself, "Well, what did you expect?" but still, it takes a little effort to enjoy (very) short stories.<br /><br />But once I was in, this book was alluring. Each story is distinguishing, its theme is completely different - and yet has something in common: uncompensated loves, hollow lives, happiness beyond grasp, the vanity of titles and brands. In these stories, I felt that there was something missing, but I couldn't quite put my finger on it - all the more fascinating, since I like stories romantic and vague.<br /><br />A lovely book for solitary moments.</span>

  <span id="freeText2984832452807625370" style="display:none">This piece is a compilation of short stories.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_427445708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/427445708" />
    <a title="Moses Kilolo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2423887-moses-kilolo"><img alt="Moses Kilolo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516734892p2/2423887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/427445708?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moses Kilolo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moses Kilolo" href="/user/show/2423887-moses-kilolo">Moses Kilolo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer427445708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9884953786574752891">Perhaps the most readable short story collection I have come across to date. Each of them makes you feel, reflect and enjoy, albeit a distilled form of human passion, pain, and desire. They are offered without the distraction of the author's emotions, but the character's deepest seated ones, rendering the writing an emotional depth worth envy, and cherish. Something light. Something worth. Something you can go back to once in a while when you have a minute or two to spare. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer599518483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9526588105094388585">My favourite book ever. You really have to like this tipe of writing because otherwise you won't like it at all. Of all the stories in the book, my personal favourite is the story about the young boy coming home and wishing that someone were there waiting for him somewhere. A must!</span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SSzzhL5xxU8ffWAW3UZhpdZd1TOrKHEXDSQhp+l4mwGS10m6QrOTgbSW7i1vPdFYx5pcos/e8ELJLfnWEXHR4g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('pWDk01SRD1C4vpRZQM7dU+Qx7yzwKTWuf46gtUy4nyV+m17tqFNZnhNVGmLytW2u9fZmvZTftPu7h3jEtLHVxg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It is the tragic monologue of a woman speaking with the child she carries in her womb. This letter confronts the burning theme of abortion, and the meaning of life, by asking difficult questions: Is it fair to impose life even if it means suffering? Would it be better not to be born at all?\nLetter to a Child Never Born touches on the real meaning of being a woman: the power to give life or not. When the book begins, the protagonist is upset after learning she is pregnant. She knows nothing about the child, except that this creature depends totally and uniquely on her own choices. The creation of another person directly within one’s own body is a very shocking thing. The sense of responsibility is huge; it is a heavy burden that gives life to endless reflections, from the origin of our existence to the shame of our selfishness. If the child could choose, would he prefer to be born, to grow up, and to suffer, or would he return to the joyful limbo from which he came? A woman’s freedom and individuality are also challenged by a newborn—should she renounce her freedom, her job, and her choice?       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda">About Anna Gavalda</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1236883498p3/26826.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda">Anna Gavalda
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,458
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3640103311676033319">Anna Gavalda is a French teacher and award-winning novelist.<br/><br/>Referred to by Voici magazine as "a distant descendant of Dorothy Parker", Anna Gavalda was born in an upper-class suburb of Paris. While working as French teacher in high school, a collection of her short stories was first published in 1999 under the title "Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part" that met with both critical acc</span>
  <span id="freeText3640103311676033319" style="display:none">Anna Gavalda is a French teacher and award-winning novelist.<br /><br />Referred to by Voici magazine as "a distant descendant of Dorothy Parker", Anna Gavalda was born in an upper-class suburb of Paris. While working as French teacher in high school, a collection of her short stories was first published in 1999 under the title "Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part" that met with both critical acclaim and commercial success, selling more than three-quarters of a million copies in her native France and winning the 2000 "Grand Prix RTL-Lire." The book was translated into numerous languages including in English and sold in twenty-seven countries. It was published to acclaim in North America in 2003 as "I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere." The book received much praise and is a library and school selection worldwide in several languages.<br /><br />Gavalda's first novel, Je l'aimais (Someone I Loved) was published in France in February 2002 and later that year in English. Inspired by the failure of her own marriage, it too was a major literary success and a bestseller and was followed by the short (96 pages) juvenile novel 35 kilos d'espoir (95 Pounds of Hope) that she said she wrote "to pay tribute to those of my students who were dunces in school but otherwise fantastic people".<br /><br />In 2004, her third novel, "Ensemble c'est tout," focused on the lives of four people living in an apartment house: a struggling young artist who works as an office cleaner at night, a young aristocrat misfit, a cook, and an elderly grandmother. The 600-page book is a bestseller in France and has been translated into English as Hunting and Gathering.<br /><br />As of 2007, her three books have sold more than 3 million copies in France. Ensemble c'est tout was made into a successful movie in 2007 by Claude Berri, with Audrey Tautou and Guillaume Canet. The adaptation of her first novel, Je l'aimais, with Daniel Auteuil and Marie-Josée Croze, was filmed in 2009 by Zabou Breitman.<br /><br />Divorced, and the mother of two, Gavalda lives in the city of Melun, Seine-et-Marne, about 50 km southeast of Paris. In addition to writing novels, she also contributes to Elle magazine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3640103311676033319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
